Match: AlwaysCoopBot vs ClaudeSonnet4_6
  Final Score: AlwaysCoopBot (27) - ClaudeSonnet4_6 (82)
  Cooperation rate: AlwaysCoopBot (100%) - ClaudeSonnet4_6 (45%)
  Round-by-round details:
    Round 1:
      - AlwaysCoopBot chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: Deterministic policy: always cooperate.
      - ClaudeSonnet4_6 chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: This is the first round with no history to go on. In a multi-round prisoner's dilemma, starting with cooperation is generally the strongest opening strategy for several reasons:

1. **Establishing a cooperative signal**: By cooperating first, I signal willingness to achieve mutual benefit (3,3) rather than triggering a defect-defect spiral (1,1). If the opponent is also playing a cooperative strategy (like Tit-for-Tat), we can sustain high payoffs throughout the game.

2. **Information gathering**: Round 1 cooperation lets me observe how the opponent responds. If they cooperate back, I know they're likely playing a cooperative strategy. If they defect, I can adjust accordingly.

3. **Expected value**: Against an unknown opponent, cooperation has a reasonable expected value. If they cooperate, I get 3. If they defect, I get 0. But defecting gives me 5 if they cooperate or 1 if they defect. Without knowing the number of rounds or opponent strategy, starting cooperative and building trust tends to outperform pure defection in iterated games.

4. **Avoiding early mutual defection lock-in**: If I defect first and they retaliate, we could get stuck in a (1,1) equilibrium for many rounds, which is worse than sustained (3,3) cooperation.

Starting with cooperation is the classic "nice" opening that tends to perform best in iterated prisoner's dilemma tournaments.
      - Payouts: AlwaysCoopBot got 3, ClaudeSonnet4_6 got 3
    Round 2:
      - AlwaysCoopBot chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: Deterministic policy: always cooperate.
      - ClaudeSonnet4_6 chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: In Round 1, both my opponent and I cooperated, resulting in a mutually beneficial outcome of 3 points each. This is a strong positive signal — my opponent demonstrated willingness to cooperate rather than defect for a short-term gain of 5.

The optimal long-term strategy in iterated prisoner's dilemma games is often a "Tit-for-Tat" or cooperative approach: reward cooperation with cooperation, and punish defection with defection. Since my opponent cooperated in Round 1, I should reciprocate with cooperation in Round 2 to:

1. **Maintain the cooperative equilibrium**: Mutual cooperation yields 3 points per round, which over many rounds far outpaces the mutual defection outcome of 1 point per round.
2. **Signal trustworthiness**: By continuing to cooperate, I signal that I'm a reliable partner, encouraging my opponent to keep cooperating.

Match: AlwaysDefectBot vs ClaudeSonnet4_6
  Final Score: AlwaysDefectBot (28) - ClaudeSonnet4_6 (18)
  Cooperation rate: AlwaysDefectBot (0%) - ClaudeSonnet4_6 (10%)
  Round-by-round details:
    Round 1:
      - AlwaysDefectBot chose 'defect'
        Reasoning: Deterministic policy: always defect.
      - ClaudeSonnet4_6 chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: This is the first round with no history to go on. In a multi-round prisoner's dilemma, starting with cooperation is generally the strongest opening strategy for several reasons:

1. **Establishing a cooperative baseline**: If the opponent is also using a cooperative or tit-for-tat strategy, mutual cooperation yields 3 points per round, which is better than mutual defection (1 point each).

2. **Signaling willingness to cooperate**: By cooperating first, I signal to the opponent that I'm open to a mutually beneficial arrangement. This can encourage them to cooperate in future rounds.

3. **Avoiding a defection spiral**: If I defect first and the opponent retaliates, we could get locked into mutual defection (1,1) which is worse than mutual cooperation (3,3).

4. **Risk assessment**: The worst case for cooperating is getting 0 (if they defect), but the best case for establishing long-term cooperation is much higher cumulative value. If this is a long game, the expected value of establishing cooperation far outweighs the short-term gain from defecting (5 vs 3).

Starting with cooperation is the classic "nice" opening in iterated prisoner's dilemma games, and strategies like Tit-for-Tat that begin cooperatively tend to perform very well in tournaments.
      - Payouts: AlwaysDefectBot got 5, ClaudeSonnet4_6 got 0
    Round 2:
      - AlwaysDefectBot chose 'defect'

        Reasoning: Tit For Tat starts by cooperating.
      - ClaudeSonnet4_6 chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: This is the first round with no history to go on. In a multi-round prisoner's dilemma, starting with cooperation is generally the strongest opening strategy for several reasons:

1. **Establishing a cooperative baseline**: If the opponent is also playing a cooperative or tit-for-tat style strategy, mutual cooperation yields 3 points per round, which is better than mutual defection (1 point each).

2. **Signaling willingness to cooperate**: By cooperating first, I signal that I'm open to a mutually beneficial relationship. This can encourage the opponent to cooperate in future rounds.

3. **Avoiding a defection spiral**: If I defect first and the opponent retaliates, we could get locked into mutual defection (1,1) which is worse than mutual cooperation (3,3).

4. **Information gathering**: Round 1 cooperation lets me observe the opponent's strategy. If they defect, I lose 3 points this round (getting 0 instead of 3), but I gain crucial information about their strategy and can adjust accordingly.

5. **Expected value**: Against an unknown opponent, cooperation has a reasonable expected value. Many rational players in iterated games recognize that cooperation is the dominant long-term strategy.

The risk is getting exploited (0 points if they defect), but the potential upside of establishing a cooperative dynamic over multiple rounds outweighs this risk.

The optimal long-term strategy in repeated prisoner's dilemma games is often "Tit-for-Tat" or cooperative strategies, especially when the opponent has shown good faith. By continuing to cooperate, I:
1. Maintain the mutually beneficial equilibrium of 3+3=6 total points per round (vs. 1+1=2 in mutual defection).
2. Signal to my opponent that I reward cooperation with cooperation, encouraging them to keep cooperating.
3. Avoid triggering a defection spiral that would lock us both into the inferior (1,1) outcome.

If I defect now, I might get 5 this round, but I risk my opponent retaliating with defection in future rounds, which would hurt my cumulative score significantly. Match: ForgivingTFT vs ClaudeSonnet4_6
  Final Score: ForgivingTFT (37) - ClaudeSonnet4_6 (57)
  Cooperation rate: ForgivingTFT (70%) - ClaudeSonnet4_6 (50%)
  Round-by-round details:
    Round 1:
      - ForgivingTFT chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: Forgiving Tit For Tat starts by cooperating.
      - ClaudeSonnet4_6 chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: This is the first round with no history to go on. In a multi-round prisoner's dilemma, starting with cooperation is generally the strongest opening strategy for several reasons:

1. **Establishing a cooperative baseline**: If the opponent is also a rational, forward-looking player, they may recognize that mutual cooperation (3,3) is better than mutual defection (1,1) over many rounds.

2. **Tit-for-tat style strategies are highly effective**: Starting with cooperation and then mirroring the opponent's previous move is one of the most robust strategies in iterated prisoner's dilemmas. By cooperating first, I signal willingness to cooperate and open the door for sustained mutual benefit.

3. **Information gathering**: Round 1 cooperation lets me observe how the opponent responds. If they cooperate back, we can potentially establish a mutually beneficial pattern. If they defect, I learn they may be aggressive and can adjust accordingly.

4. **Expected value consideration**: If the opponent is likely to cooperate (which many rational agents do in round 1), I get 3 points. If I defect and they cooperate, I get 5 — but this risks triggering retaliation in future rounds, which could cost me significantly more over time.

Starting cooperative is the strategically sound move to maximize long-term cumulative score.
      - Payouts: ForgivingTFT got 3, ClaudeSonnet4_6 got 3
    Round 2:
      - ForgivingTFT chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: Forgiving Tit For Tat: Copying opponent's cooperation.
      - ClaudeSonnet4_6 chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: In Round 1, both my opponent and I cooperated, resulting in a mutually beneficial outcome of 3 points each. This is a strong positive signal — my opponent demonstrated willingness to cooperate rather than defect for a short-term gain of 5.

The optimal long-term strategy in repeated prisoner's dilemma games is often a "Tit-for-Tat" or cooperative approach: reward cooperation with cooperation, and punish defection with defection. Since my opponent cooperated in Round 1, I should reciprocate with cooperation in Round 2 to:

1. Maintain the mutually beneficial equilibrium (3+3=6 total per round vs. 1+1=2 in mutual defection).
2. Signal to my opponent that I am a reliable cooperator, encouraging continued cooperation.
3. Avoid triggering a defection spiral that would lock us both into low payoffs.

If I defect now, I'd get 5 this round, but risk my opponent retaliating with defection in future rounds, costing me significantly over time. Sustained mutual cooperation (3 per round) outperforms the alternating or mutual defection scenarios over multiple rounds. Cooperating here is the strategically sound choice to maximize cumulative score.

Starting cooperative allows me to signal willingness to cooperate while still being able to retaliate if the opponent defects.

3. **Information gathering**: Round 1 cooperation lets me observe whether my opponent is cooperative or exploitative, which informs my strategy for subsequent rounds.

5. **Long-term maximization**: Sustained mutual cooperation (3 per round) outperforms cycles of mutual defection (1 per round) or alternating exploitation. Building trust early can lead to higher cumulative scores.

    Round 1:
      - RandomBot chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: Random bot chose cooperate.
      - ClaudeSonnet4_6 chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: This is the first round with no history to go on. In a multi-round prisoner's dilemma, starting with cooperation is generally the best opening strategy for several reasons:

1. **Establishing a cooperative baseline**: If the opponent is also a rational, forward-thinking player, they may recognize that mutual cooperation (3+3=6 total per round) is better than mutual defection (1+1=2 total per round) over many rounds.

2. **Tit-for-tat style reasoning**: Many successful strategies in iterated prisoner's dilemmas begin with cooperation and then mirror the opponent's behavior. Starting cooperative allows me to signal willingness to cooperate and potentially establish a mutually beneficial pattern.

3. **Information gathering**: By cooperating first, I can observe whether the opponent cooperates or defects, which gives me valuable information about their strategy for future rounds.

4. **Risk vs. reward**: The worst case if I cooperate and they defect is I get 0 (vs. 1 if I defect and they defect). But if we both cooperate, I get 3, which is better than the 1 from mutual defection. The upside of establishing cooperation (3 per round consistently) outweighs the one-round risk of getting 0.

Starting with cooperation is the classic "nice" opening that tends to perform well in multi-round games.

                        LLM PROMPT TEMPLATE REFERENCE
================================================================================
Below is the prompt structure sent to LLM agents during the game:

[System Prompt Template]
-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
You are agent '{agent_id}', playing a multi-round decision-making game with another player.
In each round, you and the other player must independently choose either 'cooperate' or 'defect'.
There is no communication other than seeing each other's choice history.

--- Payout Matrix ---
- You choose 'cooperate', Opponent chooses 'cooperate': You get R, Opponent gets R
- You choose 'defect', Opponent chooses 'defect': You get P, Opponent gets P
- You choose 'cooperate', Opponent chooses 'defect': You get S, Opponent gets T
- You choose 'defect', Opponent chooses 'cooperate': You get T, Opponent gets S

--- Goal ---
Maximize your own cumulative score across all rounds. Think strategically about how your choices impact future rounds.
[Optional: Custom Profile/Directives text defined in system_prompt]
-------------------------------------------------------------------------------

[User Prompt Template]
-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Current Round: {round_num}

--- Game History So Far ---
Round 1: You chose '{my_choice}', Opponent chose '{opponent_choice}'. Your payoff: X, Opponent payoff: Y.
Round 2: ...

Submit your choice for the current round ('cooperate' or 'defect').
